FOREWORD 
This report was written at the Technical University of Denmark in the Department of 
Civil Engineering as the fulfilment of the education, Master of Science. 
The thesis was written by Mikkel Korner Ewertsen and is equivalent to 30 ECTS 
points. The project has been conducted in the period from the 1st of September 
2014 to 30th of January 2015. 
I would like to thank my supervisor Lotte Bjerregaard Jensen from Department of 
Civil Engineering at Technical University of Denmark for her support, guidance and 
advice during the project. 
I also thank Morten Ørsager from Erik Møller Architects for providing the subject 
case for the thesis and the opportunity to collaborate with an architecture 
company.

MASTER THESIS 
Simulation-based assessment and design of enhanced daylight quality and 
its impact on thermal indoor environment and energy consumption, per-
formed on a housing unit in a brickwork apartment block from the 1950’s: 
Højstrupparken in Odense 
==PAGE:4==
 
iii
RESUMÉ 
Opvarmning og køling af bygninger star for ca. 40 % af energiforbruget i Danmark. 
Som en del af den politiske plan om at mindske udledningen af CO2, samt at 
konvertere fra fossile brændstoffer til vedvarende energi, er der fokus på at 
nedbringe bygningers bidrag til energiforbruget. Den eksisterende bygningsmasse 
står for størstedelen af emissionerne og disse bygninger bør bringes op til moderne 
standarder. Omkring 80 % af lejlighedsbygningerne i Danmark har murede facader, 
der er derfor behov for at udvikle bæredygtige renoveringsløsninger, som både kan 
bevare og modernisere det murede byggeri. 
Denne rapport  præsenterer en simuleringsbaseret vurdering og optimering af et 
renoveringsforslag af det almene boligbyggeri, Højstrupparken i Odense. Dette 
foregår i samarbejde med arkitektvirksomheden Erik Møller Arkitekter A/S (EMA). 
Opgavens fokus er på optimering af dagslysforhold og indeklima. Forskellige tiltag 
bliver afprøvet på en enkelt lejlighed, der skal demonstrere tegnestuens tanker og 
ideer. 
Rapporten består af løsningspakker, der er udviklet I samarbejde med EMA. 
Gennem en iterativ proces vurderes disse ved hjælp af forskellige 
simuleringsværktøjer, heriblandt IES-VE. Metoden har en holistisk tilgang og 
evaluerer: EMA’s renoveringsforslag, løsninger til reduktion af energiforbrug ved 
hjælp af enten mekanisk ventilation eller et såkaldt ventilationsvindue samt 
reduktion af overtemperaturer ved hjælp af ændret facadegeometri og ekstern 
solafskærmning. Evalueringen understøttes af en økonomisk vurdering af de 
forskellige tiltag. 
Resultaterne af simuleringerne viser at isolering af gavl, nye vinduer og mekanisk 
ventilationsanlæg kan sænke energiforbruget med 69 %. Ændringer af lejlighedens 
rumfordeling samt flytning af vinduer i gavlen resulterede i en stigning af 
dagslysfaktoren på 24 % i forhold til de eksisterende forhold. Ændringer i 
facadegeometri kombineret med udvendig solafskærmning reducerede perioder 
med for høje temperature i sommerperioder. Beregninger af energisparepriser viste 
at samtlige tiltag har en lavere omkostning end prisen på fjernvarme, hvorfor disse 
anses som værende økonomisk rentable. 
Det konstateres at der er brug for en mere videnskabelig og analytisk tilgang til 
renoveringen af eksisterende bygninger, hvis energiforbruget skal sænkes samtidig 
med at der opretholdes et acceptabelt indeklima. EMA’s renoveringsforlag er 
baseret på antagelser og det er derfor nødvendigt at etablere oplysninger om de 
eksisterende forhold inden designprocessen for at kunne foretage valg baseret på 
viden. Optimalt bør dette udføres I starten af designfasen således at denne viden 
kan danne grundlag for et design der både reducerer energiforbruget og øger 
kvaliteten af indeklimaet. 
 
==PAGE:5==
 
iv
ABSTRACT 
Heating and cooling of buildings account to around 40 % of the energy usage in 
the Danish society. As a part of a conversion from fossil fuels to sustainable energy, 
there is a political focus on reducing buildings contribution to the energy usage. As 
the existing buildings stands for the majority of the emissions, focus is therefore on 
these in order to bring them up to modern standards. Around 80 % of the Danish 
buildings have masonry facades. There is a need to develop sustainable renovation 
solutions that can both preserve and modernise masonry buildings.  
This thesis presents a simulation based assessment and optimisation of a 
renovation proposal of the public housing Højstrupparken in Odense in 
collaboration with the architecture company Erik Møller Architects (EMA). Focus is 
on optimisation of daylight and indoor climate in a comprehensive energy 
renovation project, tested on a single apartment.  
The report consist of ‘packages’ of solutions developed together with the architect, 
which is then assessed through iterations, using different kinds of simulations tools 
including IES-VE. The method is based on a holistic approach and evaluates; 
EMA’s proposal, reduction of energy consumption by the means of a ventilation 
window and a mechanical ventilation system, and reduction of indoor temperatures 
by the means of different façade geometry and an external shading device. This is 
supported by an economic assessment of the various initiatives. 
Simulation results of the different packages showed that insulation in the gable and 
new windows combined with a mechanical ventilation system were able to reduce 
the energy consumption with 69 %. With internal changes to the floor plan and a 
rearrangement of the windows in the gable, it was possible to obtain an increase in 
daylight factor in the apartment with up to 24 % compared to the existing 
conditions. Changes to the façade geometry combined with an external shading 
device was able to reduce too high temperatures in summer periods. Costs of 
conserved energy were found to be below the price of district heating and the 
different packages was therefore found to be economically profitable. 
It was found that, if the requirements to lower the energy consumption of the 
existing buildings are to be met, there is a need to take a more scientific and 
analytical approach to the renovation designs and ideas before they are built. 
EMA’s renovation method is based on assumptions. It is therefore necessary to 
establish information about the existing conditions, which should optimally be 
performed in the beginning of the design phase, where the results can be used as 
basis for a design that complies with the goals of reducing energy while increasing 
the quality for the users of the buildings and their experience of the indoor 
environment.

1 INTRODUCTION 
There is a political focus on climate changes and to reduce CO2 emissions radically 
towards 2050, where our energy shall come from renewable sources (Klima- & 
Energiministeriet, 2011). 
A part of this is focused on investments in green energy and the other part is 
optimisation of current technology and infrastructure. Heating and cooling of 
buildings accounts for approx. 40 % of the energy usage in the Danish society 
(Klima- & Energiministeriet, 2011). It is not possible to reach the governments 
ambitions of a carbon neutral society only by building new and energy efficient 
houses as these only accounts for a fraction of the building mass. It is therefore 
necessary to focus on the existing buildings in order to bring these up to modern 
standards.  
Around 80 % of the Danish buildings have masonry facades (Wittchen & Kragh, 
2013). It is a material that through history has proven to be durable, and it gives our 
cities and buildings a unique identity, forming a part of our cultural heritage. There 
is a need to develop sustainable renovation solutions that preserves and develops 
both the architectural qualities of masonry buildings while minimising energy 
consumption. In the process it is also important to look at other aspects that can 
increase well-being in our homes, as these tend to have an impact on our overall 
life quality. Indoor climate is one of the major stakeholders concerning our health, 
thus reduction in energy consumption and quality of indoor climate must go hand 
in hand. 
Daylight is also an important factor for both household energy consumption and 
life- and health-quality for the people who live in the dwellings, and it is therefore an 
important part of renovation and energy optimisation of our building stock. 
1.1 AIM OF THE PROJECT 
The aim of the project is to simulate different design scenarios of a renovation 
project of a masonry apartment building in Højstrupparken, Odense. The outcome 
of the analysis will aid the architects in the early design phases of the renovation 
project, and optimize the amounts of daylights while ensuring an acceptable indoor 
climate and a reduction in energy consumption. The report consist of development 
of ‘packages’ of solutions developed together with the architect, which is then 
assessed through iterations, using different kinds of simulations tools including IES-
VE. The report will be concluded with recommendations for both actual solutions 
but also generic design processes for this kind of projects based on continuous 
reflections in the process. The results can be used as a tool for the architects to 
visualize and communicate the effects of design solutions on the daylight and 
indoor climate. 
==PAGE:10==
  
 
 
 
2
1.2 PROJECT CONTENT 
Erik Møller Architects is developing a proposal for a renewal of the apartment 
buildings in Højstrupparken, Odense. The project shall ensure sustainable 
renovation solutions with a high architectural quality. The project works as a 
benchmark for future renovation solutions for masonry buildings.  
Following their win of the design competition there is a need of an in-depth analysis 
of their different design ideas, as well as an exploration of the process as such. 
This thesis will focus on daylight and thermal indoor environment. Simulations of 
the existing conditions in a representative apartment unit, forms the basis for the 
analysis of the design proposals. In addition to verifying the already developed 
design proposal, this thesis seeks to optimize these designs to decrease the 
energy demand while maintaining an acceptable thermal indoor environment that 
complies with the Danish building regulations. 
 
 
==PAGE:11==
  
 
 
 
3
2 BACKGROUND 
2.1 INCENTIVES FOR RENOVATION 
2.1.1 Political incentives 
In 2009 the Danish government started the climate commission, whose mission 
was to come up with suggestions on how Denmark can become independent from 
fossil fuels (Klimakommissionen, 2010). The commission’s work has resulted in a 
blueprint for the political ambitions and strategies to reach the target of a fossil fuel 
free society in 2050 (Klima- & Energiministeriet, 2011). Fossil fuels constitute 
around 80 % of the energy consumption in Denmark. A large amount of these fuels 
are imported, and the numbers are set to increase if nothing is changed. Burning of 
fossil fuels is assumed to be the main contributor to the climate changes that are 
currently experienced (Klima- & Energiministeriet, 2011). 
It is a political objective to become independent of fossil fuels while reducing CO2 
emissions. The energy savings shall amongst other initiatives be found in the 
building sector. Building energy consumption must be halved in 2050, by the 
means of, amongst other, renovating 75 % of the poorest insulated buildings 
(Thomsen et al., 2012). It is also estimated that buildings constructed after 2020 
will be energy neutral and that 15 % of the energy consumption shall come from 
solar energy (Thomsen et al., 2012). Heating of buildings shall be performed with 
district heating and electrical heat pumps driven by windmills. The district heating 
shall primarily consist of large heat pumps, solar heating systems and geothermal 
systems with storage capacity for periods with no wind or sun. Biomass shall be 
used to assist the sustainable energy in periods where the energy production is too 
low. The political ambition is that heating of buildings shall be performed with 
primarily sustainable energy in 2035 (Klima- & Energiministeriet, 2011).  
Construction of new buildings constitutes to 1 % of the building mass, and 
demolition of worn out buildings is equally low. Unless we start tearing buildings 
down instead, this means that most of the buildings that exist today will still be 
there in 2050 (Klima- Energi & Bygningsministeriet, 2014). Renovation of existing 
buildings is therefore a significant step towards the goals set by the government, 
which makes this an important topic on the political agenda.  
There is large energy savings connected to renovating the existing building mass. It 
is assumed profitable when energy savings measures are implemented in 
connection to renovation or replacement of components. Buildings have a long 
lifetime and many of the building components have a lifetime between 30-50 years. 
It is therefore important that effective solutions are implemented as soon as 
possible. It is necessary to perform initiatives to reduce barriers for investing in 
energy reducing measures. Changes to the building regulations can also change 
==PAGE:12==
  
 
 
 
4
the direction and pace of the implementation by tightening the requirements to 
components and renovations. 
2.1.2 Economical incentives 
Buildings are a vital capital stock in the economy and they must be maintained in 
order to keep their value. A societal concern is that the maintenance debt of the 
building stock is growing too large, as this can cause significant expenses for 
existing or future building owners. Many buildings are affected by loans and 
mortgages and it is therefore essential that the value is corresponding to these 
(Havelund et al., 2013).  
The building stock is functioning as a value chain where production, operation and 
maintenance produce the values. Renovation is also a possibility to implement 
contemporary solutions, decrease energy demand or increase the architectural and 
utilitarian value. When properly maintained, buildings have a long lifetime, but if a 
building has a large maintenance debt, it can be too costly to improve the building 
to modern standards. In these situations, an economical analysis of renovation vs. 
building new must be made. In some cases it can be economically beneficial to 
build a new building and reduce the costs for maintenance in a period of 20-30 
years (Havelund et al., 2013).  
Building renovation is closely linked to the development of property value, and 
population movement. The tendency is that more people move towards the larger 
cities, which drives the prices up and thereby increases incentives for renovation of 
buildings in these areas. 
2.2 A HOLISTIC APPROACH TO BUILDING RENOVATION 
Around 50 % of the Danish building stock is from the period 1930-1979, which is 
before regulations were introduced to reduce energy consumption. Together with a 
high energy consumption for heating there are also other challenges such as out-
dated floor plans, moisture and mould problems caused by a bad indoor climate.  
Most energy renovations are modest renovations, where only a couple of building 
components are changed or optimised. A report from Niras (Niras, 2013) deals with 
the practical experiences with energy renovations and shows that approx. 2/3 of 
the performed renovations are part solutions, where some of the components and 
installations are changed. 1/3 is comprehensive renovations that cover large parts 
of the building and its installations. The most performed renovations are windows, 
roofs and façades covering 60, 58 and 56 % of the renovations. Ventilation 
systems are optimised or changed in around 28 %. 
The comprehensive renovation process is therefore more complicated than just 
changing or improving some components in order to reduce the energy 
consumption. The indoor climate has to be taken into account as well. A holistic 
==PAGE:13==
  
 
 
 
5
approach is therefore needed in order to cope with the challenges regarding 
energy consumption and improvement of the usability of the apartments (Eriksen, 
Rode, & Bjarløv, 2013). 
Holistic renovation covers health, well-being, architecture, energy and economy 
(Kongebro, 2012). This differs from “normal” renovation, where the primary topics 
are energy and economy. Health and well-being are covering topics such as indoor 
climate, daylight access, access to outdoor living areas etc. The different topics 
interferes, therefore it is not possible to only adjust on parameters in one category. 
Energy and architecture are closely linked. It is estimated that 40-50 % of a 
building’s energy consumption is locked to the actual design. Validation and 
optimisation of the early phases of the design is therefore the cheapest and most 
effective way of reducing the energy demand of a new building (Kongebro, 
Jørgensen, Nielsen, & Strømann-Andersen, 2012). In renovation however, the 
geometry is to some extent defined from the beginning as the building is already 
there.  
Economy is a major part of the decision process towards an investment in building 
renovation. For building owners it is often the decisive factor for the scope of the 
renovation. In order to use a holistic process, engineers and architects must learn 
to work together in the early phases of design to produce better and more 
sustainable buildings. A good building is a building where the five aspects work in 
synergy and mutually reinforce each other (Kongebro et al., 2012). 
2.3 INDOOR CLIMATE 
The indoor climate is by WHO divided into five categories, which are: 
• 
Thermal (temperature, air speed, draughts etc.) 
• 
Atmospheric (air quality, C02 etc.)  
• 
Visual (Daylight, electric light, view etc.) 
• 
Acoustic (Noise, noise reduction) 
• 
Mechanic (ergonomics etc.)  
It is especially the thermal, atmospheric and visual indoor climate that affects a 
building’s energy consumption. 
Indoor climate is more than pleasant temperatures, required air exchanges and 
energy consumption. People spend approximately 80-90 % of their time indoors 
(Christoffersen, 2005), and the occupant’s perception of the indoor climate is 
therefore a major contributor to ensuring a good indoor environment. The user’s 
experience of autonomy of control of the building is essential for a good perception 
==PAGE:14==
  
 
 
 
6
of the indoor climate. Possibilities such as control of solar shading, setting the 
ventilation temperature and speed are factors that have a great effect on the 
occupant. Persons have a higher tolerance of fluctuations in temperatures etc. 
when they feel that they have the possibility to control the indoor conditions 
(Kongebro et al., 2012). An acceptance of these fluctuations can contribute to a 
reduction of the energy consumption to keep a pleasant indoor climate. 
2.3.1 Requirements for indoor temperatures 
“DS-EN15251” (Dansk Standard, 2007) indicates parameters for different classes 
of indoor environment. For the simulations of the thermal indoor environment of the 
existing conditions, category 3 is used as guideline for input parameters. Category 
3 is described as being applicable for existing buildings.  
The ambition for the renovation proposals is to comply with category 2, which is 
the normal level of expectation to indoor climate and is used for new and renovated 
buildings. Category 4 is for values that do not fit the criteria of the other categories. 
This category should only be accepted for a limited part of the year (Dansk 
Standard, 2007, p. 15). Table A.2 states that the operative temperature shall not 
exceed 27°C in summer periods and the minimum temperature during winter shall 
be higher than 18°C (Dansk Standard, 2007). In addition DS 474 (Dansk Standard, 
1995) allows exceeding of the design temperatures for a prolonged period. The 
primary target is to comply with the Danish building regulations, and hereafter to 
comply with category 2. In some areas, such as the ventilation rate, the 
requirement is different between Danish standard and the building regulations.  
The design criteria for the allowed number of hours above the design temperatures 
are given in Table 2.1.  
Table 2.1 
Design limits (Dansk 
Standard, 1995). 
 
 
Temp. >26°C [h] 
Temp. >27°C [h] 
Maximum limit 
100 
25 
 
2.3.2 Prediction of the perceived indoor climate 
Predicted mean vote (PMV) and Predicted People Dissatisfied (PPD), developed by 
P.A. Fanger in 1973 (Fanger, 1973), are used to determine a persons perception of 
the indoor climate based on humidity, air temperature, mean radiant temperature, 
air speed and the occupants level of activity and clothing (Madsen, 1998). The PMV 
index indicates the amount of general thermal discomfort experienced on a scale 
from -3 to 3, see Figure 2.1. The PPD index is a supplement to PMV, as this 
indicates the amount of people who will be unsatisfied with the thermal indoor 
climate based on the PMV index. Figure 2.1 shows the relation between PMV and 
PPD.  
==PAGE:15==
  
 
 
 
7
Figure 2.1 
Relation between PMV 
and PPD (Madsen, 1998) 
 
 
The figure also shows that it can be expected that at least 5 % will feel dissatisfied 
with the thermal indoor climate caused by personal preferences, amount of clothing 
or activity level. The indoor climate is considered acceptable if PPD is less than 20 
% (Dansk Standard, 1995). 
2.4 DAYLIGHT AND ITS EFFECT ON HUMANS 
Daylight is one of the strongest tools to develop an architectural experience of a 
space. It is desired because it both fulfils the need to see and to experience 
environmental stimulation. The properties of daylight means that it provides the 
best conditions for viewing and working because it offers a high illuminance and 
very good colour rendering (Ruck et al., 2000). It can furthermore be used to create 
a good indoor climate and daylight conditions for the residents. Daylight does more 
than illuminating rooms; it has influence on the human body and brain.  
Humans are adopted to live in an outside environment, and all functions of the 
body are optimised to work in natural conditions. Modern lifestyle is very different 
from this, as almost all activity is performed indoors or in different transportation 
systems. This lack of natural light can be a cause for many of the modern deceases 
such as seasonal affective disorder, depression etc. Direct sunlight is often stated 
as the primary cause for skin cancer but the main reason shall be found in the 
amount of time people spend in the sun in their vacations compared to their daily 
life, where they spend most of their time indoors.  
Daylight also has an impact on neural and hormonal signals. The production of 
melatonin is highly affected by daylight (Jacobs, 2004). Melatonin controls the sleep 
cycle, and is produced when a person is situated in a dark place. When the eye is 
exposed to bright light with an intensity above 1500 lux, the production of 
==PAGE:16==
  
 
 
 
8
melatonin stops. This makes daylight more effective than known substitutes such 
as coffee etc. Disturbances to the sleep cycle can severely affect the body and is a 
major cause for the before mentioned deceases (Christoffersen, 2005; Jacobs, 
2004).   
Daylight should be the primary light source during the day, both in order to reduce 
energy consumption and for the health and well being of the users of the building. 
In order to use daylight as the main light source, it is necessary to control it with 
shading devices. An increased amount of daylight has a negative effect on the 
thermal indoor climate as direct sunlight contributes to overheating of the rooms. A 
retractable external shading devise is the most effective solution to decrease direct 
sunlight and ensure a good thermal indoor environment (Christoffersen, 2005). 
2.4.1 Requirements for daylight in residential building 
The building regulations Ch. 6.5.2 requires that living spaces are well lit. This can 
be achieved by having a window to floor area ratio of at least 10 %, with a light 
transmittance through the windows of at least 75 %. Alternatively the daylight can 
be calculated or simulated. If this method is used, the daylight factor (DF) shall be 
at least 2 % in half of the room (Erhvervs- og byggestyrelsen, 2009). However, new 
research indicates that the required daylight factor should be higher in order to 
increase the natural light in buildings and the health benefits that follows (Volf, 
2014).  
 
 
 
==PAGE:17==
  
 
 
 
9
3 METHOD AND ASSUMPTIONS 
The method for this thesis consists of 4 phases.  
• 
Establishment of knowledge of existing conditions  
• 
Evaluation of EMA’s design proposal  
• 
Optimisation of proposal with focus on indoor climate and daylight 
• 
Economical comparison of the found solutions.  
These phases form the basis for a holistic comparison of the different proposals in 
order to find the solutions that combined satisfies most of the parameters in the 
holistic approach. 
3.1 ESTABLISH A BASIS FOR COMPARISON 
The first phase of the project is to establish a reference. It is essential to establish 
information about the performance of the existing conditions as the design 
proposals and optimisations has to be compared to the existing conditions. Tools 
for this phase are data from the building owner regarding building components, 
drawings, energy label reports etc. On this background it is possible to make 
simulations and establish a comparative baseline for both indoor climate and 
energy consumption. The amount and type of solutions is also defined by the 
existing conditions as renovation proposals should increase the qualities of the 
building compared to the existing conditions. However, examination of the existing 
conditions proactively outlines problems and design solutions. If the existing 
solution has problems with overtemperatures, this can guide the design in a 
direction informed by this knowledge. 
3.2 EVALUATION OF DESIGN 
On the basis of the material provided by the architecture company, simulations are 
made on both indoor climate and daylight levels. The evaluation gives the company 
in idea of how their proposed solutions performs compared to the existing 
conditions, thus creating a higher level of documented effects of the design 
proposals. The design process can be viewed as a series of continuous iterations. 
3.3 OPTIMISATION OF DESIGN 
The optimisation process of the design is following a method as shown in Figure 
3.1, with the three steps; reduction through design, optimisation through technical 
solutions and production through sustainable energy production. In renovation a 
large part of the energy reducing design is already defined in the geometry, 
orientation etc. If the building is listed or the municipality has put limitations to 
==PAGE:18==
  
 
 
 
10
changes to the façade or overall look of the building it will shift the areas of triangle 
in different directions and put more emphasis on the other parameters.  
Figure 3.1 
Three practical tools 
in sustainable 
building design 
(Kongebro et al., 
2012)  
 
 
The different parameters are then used in the evaluation. The relation between the 
parameters is complex and changes to one parameter can have both positive and 
negative consequences for others. Through an iterative process, different solutions 
are tested and evaluated with the architecture company. 
Different solutions to reduce energy consumption and improve indoor climate are 
tested, including different window niche designs and external shading devices. 
During the optimisation, different solutions to decrease heat loss through ventilation 
are tested, including a solution with a ventilation window. Earlier projects have 
developed a mathematical expression to describe heating of air through a cavity 
between the glass layers in the window (Laustsen et al., 2014). Here, this method is 
used in order to find an expression for the case apartment. The found 
mathematical expression is implemented in IES-VE and used to estimate the 
effects of the solution on energy consumption and indoor climate. In addition, a 
mechanical system is tested in order to compare the ventilation window against a 
commonly known product.  
3.4 ECONOMICAL COMPARISON OF SOLUTIONS 
An economical comparison is included in order to support a holistic evaluation of 
the renovation solutions.  
The building regulations require that profitable initiatives are conducted. Some 
constructions and initiatives are not profitable from the perspective of the building 
regulations, but they can be seen as a contributor to lowering the energy 
consumption of the building. The profitability demand from the building regulations 
can in some cases result in misleading conclusions and be a barrier for 
Produce 
Optimise 
Reduce 
==PAGE:19==
  
 
 
 
11
implementing beneficial renovation initiatives. Cost of saved energy (CSE) is chosen 
as the economical evaluation tool as it includes more parameters. The following 
section describes the calculation method for CSE.  
3.4.1 Calculation of prices 
The different solutions’ prices are calculated or obtained from viable sources. The 
prices are considered as estimates that function as a guidance tool in order to have 
a basis for the evaluation. Prices are primarily obtained through suppliers and 
contractors. If that is not possible, calculations are performed with the program 
“V&S Prisdata 2014”. Prices calculated in this program include salary, materials and 
garbage disposal.  
As the scope of this thesis is on a single apartment, the economical evaluation will 
be from a consumer economic point of view and all prices are therefore considered 
to include VAT. 
3.4.2 Investment factor 
The need for renovation of a building component is taken into account to 
compensate the calculations in case of a part is exchanged before its technical 
lifetime has been reached. The energy investment factor (EIF) (Tommerup, 2010) is 
a factor based on the remaining lifetime of a component compared to its technical 
lifetime: 
!"# = !"#$%&%&'!!"#$!!"#$!!"!!"#$"%&%'
!"#ℎ!"#$%!!"#$%"&$!!"!!"#$"%&%'  
EIF is multiplied on the investment in a new component. Annex 6 Table 2 from the 
building regulations (Erhvervs- og byggestyrelsen, 2009) and www.levetider.dk 
(Heinz, Hansen, & Grundejernes Investeringsfond, 2015) are used for the estimation 
of lifetimes and remaining lifetimes. 
3.4.3 Cost of saved energy 
The method is taking parameters such as lifetime, expenses for loans, energy 
investment and savings, and future development of energy prices into 
consideration. By taking a large number of parameters into account it enables a 
more precise prediction of the profitability of a specific renovation solution than 
methods such as profitability and present-value calculations (Tommerup & 
Svendsen, 2008):

CSE describes how much it costs to save 1 kWh. If an energy reducing initiative is 
profitable it must have a CSE that is lower than the local energy price. It is also 
possible to compare different solutions and rank them after their CSE. 
3.5 COLLABORATION WITH ARCHITECTURE COMPANY 
Erik Møller Architects has been included during the process of analysing the case 
building. The aim of the collaboration is to connect in an interdisciplinary project 
work with the company. As it is one of the skills that newly educated engineers 
from architectural engineering is trained for, it shall function as a test for a real life 
situation where the student is collaborating with the architectural company as a 
technical consultant from the early design phase. To keep the company informed 
there is arranged evaluation meetings during the project period, where the found 
solutions and results are presented and discussed with the architects. The 
meetings are conducted informally and the material is being presented with graphs 
or diagrams that clearly illustrates the engineer’s thoughts.  
3.6 SIMULATION TOOLS 
Documentation of the energy consumption and indoor climate is based on 
simulations performed in the program “IES-VE 2014”. The daylight distribution and 
daylight factors are investigated with the program “Velux Visualizer 2”. The models 
used in “Visualizer” are produced with “Trimble Sketchup”. Calculations of U-values 
for building components are performed with “Rockwool Energy Design”.  
Table 3.1 shows a list of the used software together with a description and version 
of the program.

3.7 ASSUMPTIONS 
In this thesis a single apartment is tested in order to see how different solutions 
affect the indoor climate and energy consumption. This is done to inform the 
architecture company about the consequences of their choices and not to inform 
about an overall energy frame of the building. The found energy savings are net 
savings, and no factors are added. They function as guidelines for the expected 
savings that can be obtained by the different design solutions. The energy frame for 
the whole building is seen as irrelevant for this thesis as the aim is to look at how a 
number of solutions affect a single apartment that is used for demonstration of 
proposals that, at a later point, can be used in the process of renovating the entire 
area of Højstrupparken.  
In this phase of the project, where the scope is a single apartment, it is therefore 
assumed that the energy savings are the most informative tool. At a later point, 
when some solutions shall be implemented in the entire project, it is advisable to 
look at the overall energy frame for the building. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:22==
  
 
 
 
14
4 CASE BUILDING 
Figure 4.1 
Overview of 
Højstrupparken in 
Odense. (Source: 
www.kpf.dk) 
 
 
The case building is a part of the building complex Højstrupparken located in 
Odense west. Architect Erik Eriksen and landscape architect C. Th. Sørensen 
designed and built Højstrupparken in the period from 1948 to 1952 (KPF Arkitekter, 
2013). It is a typical example from the time period of general housing with a high 
architectural value. The building complex forms a simple and harmonious whole 
that is well incorporated with the surrounding park area. The facades are in red 
bricks with red tinted mortar and a red pantile roof. Entrance areas and balconies 
are made of white painted concrete elements with a high level of detailing, which 
adds to the characteristics of the building complex. One of the architectural 
highlights is the balcony / bay window solution that gives a sculptural relief on the 
façade with horizontal and vertical lines of shade and light. Furthermore these add 
to the functional qualities of the apartments as they act as a fusion between inside 
and outside (Høi et al., 2013). 
Figure 4.2 
Højstrupparken, building 
blocks (source: 
www.fabbo.dk) 
 
 
==PAGE:23==
  
 
 
 
15
4.1 RELEVANCE TO THESIS 
95 % of apartment buildings in Denmark are in brickwork and a large amount of 
these needs to be renovated in order to comply with the government’s ambition of 
lower CO2 emissions and energy consumption from buildings (Wittchen, 2004).  
Realdania, State Building Fund and the Property Owners Investment Fund has 
chosen to focus on the 1940’s and 1950’s brickwork housing in order to develop 
methods to secure the brickwork building culture for the future. Their vision is to 
create new knowledge, solutions and building components that are transferrable to 
other renovation projects. In 2013 Højstrupparken was chosen as a demonstration 
project of Realdania’s campaign due to its architectural value and its compliance 
with the campaign requirements. Højstrupparken is facing a comprehensive 
renovation with a budget of 490 million DKK. This includes energy optimisation of 
the buildings and modernisation of the apartments (Realdania, 2013). In the 
beginning of 2015 a demonstration project is supposed to be build. It will consist of 
a single apartment that will showcase some of the proposed technologies and 
renovation proposals for the project. 
It is in this context that Højstrupparken has relevance for this thesis as it represents 
a platform for an analytical and scientific approach to the renovation process 
together with an architectural focus on preserving the existing qualities of the 
buildings and surroundings.  
4.2 CONTENTS OF COMPETITION PROGRAM 
The aim of the competition is to ensure that Højstrupparken can serve as an 
attractive residential area in the future while ensuring the architectural and cultural 
heritage as the building area represents. The main themes of the competition are 
interior design and energy renovation. On this basis the contenders shall come up 
with solutions on three areas: 
• 
The relationship between the park and the apartments. Renewal of the 
balconies and bay windows in a way that strengthens the existing 
characters of the building together with increased accessibility and 
reduced thermal bridges from the construction 
• 
Establish elevator access to apartments, and solutions to solve this without 
compromising the apartments. 
• 
Add qualities that increase the quality of the apartments. These qualities 
can be increased daylight, with light from both sides, better indoor climate, 
comfort etc. (KPF Arkitekter, 2013). 
==PAGE:24==
  
 
 
 
16
As Højstrupparken is a general housing unit, it is important that the diversity of the 
residents is maintained. The solutions have to be flexible in order to accommodate 
a number of different residents, from young to families and old people.  
There are a large number of constructional problems that are related to thermal 
bridges. These can cause problems with mould growth and comfort. It is essential 
that these issues be addressed in a manner that respects the buildings architecture 
and style. 
4.3 BUILDING STYLES OF THE 1940’S AND 1950’S BRICKWORK 
BUILDINGS 
The transition from neoclassicism to functionalism began around 1930. Walter 
Gropius and Le Corbusier are seen as some of the international pioneers for the 
building style with an architectural focus on practicality and rationality. Concrete, 
steel and glass were the main building materials, and these materials where joined 
in “honest” constructions without redundant decorations. Arne Jacobsen is an 
example of a Danish architect that brought the international functionalism to 
Denmark with the Bellavista multi-storey buildings in Klampenborg. In this period 
there existed a Danish and more traditional direction of functionalism, with focus on 
local materials and building methods (Gyldendal, 2014). C.F Møller and Kay Fisker 
are seen as prominent architects of traditional functionalism with their brickwork 
buildings in Copenhagen. Examples of international and traditional functionalism are 
shown in Figure 4.3. 
Figure 4.3 
Examples of international 
and traditional 
functionalism. Left: 
Bellavista. Right: 
Dronningegården 
(www.arkitekturbilleder.dk
)  
 
In the period from 1930-1950 a large number of park buildings was built in 
traditional functionalism, with red or yellow brickwork facades, large green grass 
areas, playgrounds and shared facilities in a landscaped architectural concept. 
Brickwork communicates well with the surroundings and gives these buildings an 
aesthetic value and a large degree of preservation value. The brickwork was the 
most significant building material, and represents the material expression and 
cultural identity of buildings from the period (Bech-Danielsen et al., 2011).  
Common for buildings from this period is that there hasn’t been focus on insulation 
and heat losses. Form and function of the apartments were the primary focus as 
==PAGE:25==
  
 
 
 
17
well as craftsmanship and materials. Daylight and access to the outside was an 
integrated part of the design, as this was a large contributor to the function of the 
apartments.  
It is estimated that apartment buildings from the period has an overall heat loss of 
10,7 PJ which equals 20% of the total heat loss for all apartment buildings 
(Wittchen, 2004). Multi storey buildings generally have a relatively low heat loss 
compared to other building types. They have a large heated floor area relative to 
the climate envelope. Figure 4.4 shows the distributed heat losses of multi storey 
buildings from the period 1930-1950 and it is visible that the main contributors to 
the heat loss is outer walls and ventilation. The ventilation is based on a natural air 
exchange on approx. 0,5 h-1.  
Figure 4.4 
Heat losses of Danish 
multi storey buildings from 
the period 1930-1950 
(Wittchen, 2004) 
 
 
4.4 CONSERVATION VALUES 
The main conservation values are linked to the cultural and architectural qualities 
that characterises Højstrupparken. 
4.4.1 Cultural preservation values 
The societal, cultural and political movements in Denmark in the post-war period 
are clearly visible in Højstrupparken. The master plan, building design and 
disposition tells the story about the period, as do the building materials and 
methods. The clear context between the buildings and the landscape tells about 
the period’s focus on light and air and better apartments. This is visible in the bay 
windows and balconies that are oriented after the sunlight. Elements from the park 
reflect the architects wish for logical and beautiful environments for the residents. 
The buildings are from a time where there were developed new and innovative 
construction solutions such as prefab concrete slabs and balconies. The brickwork 
facades and the element-cast details tells a story about the period’s craftsmanship 
Roof!

built in the period 1930-1950  
==PAGE:26==
  
 
 
 
18
and constructional development, and this is also a part of the cultural preservation 
value (Høi et al., 2013). 
4.4.2 Architectural preservation values 
The architectural preservation values are closely linked to the correlation between 
landscape, buildings and gardens together with the successful interaction between 
the three building sections and its different gardens. Despite the differences 
between the building sections, Højstrupparken forms a gathered and integrated 
building complex with spatial qualities. The red brickwork facades and red pantile 
roofs are consequently used for all the buildings. Construction parts such as 
balconies, entrance canopies and blinds are also helping to tie the different building 
types together. The window types clearly reflect their intended use with different 
window types for living room, bathroom and kitchen. 
The facades, with their characteristic red bricks, with relief and red mortar appear 
with simple decorations dictated by the construction and the low curved 
connectors are hallmarks for the building complex. The large brickwork surfaces, 
together with the white cast concrete elements are an essential value for the 
building complex. The roof is of newer date, with added windows and is differing 
from the original layout. But they still appear as a homogenous surface that 
connects the buildings together, and these are also of significant preservation value 
(Høi et al., 2013). 
Højstrupparken is a valuable part of the telling about the post-war buildings and is a 
well preserved example of general housing in Denmark 
==PAGE:27==
  
 
 
 
19
4.5 EXISTING CONDITIONS 
Figure 4.5 
Plan drawing of 
demonstration apartment, 
existing conditions. (See 
app. 1) 
 
 
The plan layout of the demonstration apartment is shown in Figure 4.5. The 
apartment consists of a dining room with a small balcony in the gable (room 03), a 
living room with balcony and bay window (room 01), a bedroom (room 02) and a 
small kitchen (room 07) and bathroom.  
4.5.1 Climate envelope 
Except from windows, doors and roof, the building envelope is unchanged since it 
was build. The majority of the outer walls are solid brick, reinforced in window 
rebates and lintels with steel-concrete columns or beams. ½ stone brick covers 
these. Brick parapets are with radiator niches and are constructed in ½ stone 
thickness covered with 20 mm cork insulation on the inside. Gables are cavity walls 
with masonry ties. There are a large number of thermal bridges in the façade 
construction caused by the solid brick walls, reinforcements, binders, balconies 
etc. Table 4.1 gives an overview of the U-values for the building elements.

The roof has been extensively renovated in 1998. The roof is covered by pantiles

All apartments have floors against other apartments or unheated basements. Some 
apartments have floors over open doorways. There are basements under all 
building blocks. These are unheated (Fritsdal, 2009).

4.5.2 Installations 
The apartments are naturally ventilated and with exhaust vents in kitchens. Some 
kitchens have mechanical hoods with exhausts through the façade. There are no 
exhaust vents from bathrooms; these are also naturally ventilated as they all have 
windows. 
Heating of the building block is performed with district heating. There is no data on 
the age or type of the heating aggregates. The apartments are heated with 
radiators placed under the windows and the heat distribution system is original with 
insulated pipes outside apartments (Fritsdal, 2009).  
4.6 ERIK MØLLER ARCHITECTS’ RENOVATION PROPOSAL 
Erik Møller Architects (hereafter EMA) has been chosen as winner of the 
competition for the best renovation proposals for Højstrupparken. The main targets 
for Realdania and the residents of Højstrupparken are: 
• 
Modernise Højstrupparken with respect to the existing qualities 
• 
Improve the apartments functionality and accessibility 
==PAGE:29==
  
 
 
 
21
• 
Energy renovation in harmony with the original architecture and as a role 
model for the ‘40s and ‘50s brickwork buildings 
• 
Good building solutions 
• 
New knowledge, solutions and building components 
EMA has chosen a holistic approach to the task and has defined it as finding the 
balance between residents’ welfare, overall economic sense, minimal 
environmental impact and the existing qualities of brick buildings. 
The building analysis addresses the buildings conservation values, the apartments 
functionality, energy renovation and indoor climate.  
With a focus on the apartments functionality they have proposed a revised balcony 
/ bay window solution with better accessibility for impaired people and more space 
for using the balcony. This is done with respect to the original balcony design that 
is a characteristic part of Højstrupparken and classified as having a preservation 
value. The original balcony / bay window solution has a bay window that is wider 
than the balcony, and gives the balcony a sleek look from the outside. EMA want to 
retain the solution, but with better accessibility. This results in the balconies being 
wider than the bay window, but the same placement will create equal shadows and 
reliefs on the facades as seen in Figure 4.6. On the gables of the buildings there are 
also placed balconies within the façade. These are very small and enclosed. EMA 
wants to include the existing balconies in the living space and avoid some of the 
thermal bridges from the balcony construction. By removing the balconies, they 
move the window surface forward into the surface allowing more daylight and solar 
heat into the apartments. 
Figure 4.6 
Left: EMA’s solution. 
Right: The original 
solution (Erik Møller 
Arkitekter, 2012) 
 
 
Another of their key proposals is the introduction of a more open floor plan that 
allows daylight to be easily distributed in the apartment. The original concept was 
rooms with functional divisions, creating small rooms with limited daylight access. 
As both kitchen and bathroom face an extensive renovation, it was therefore ideal 
==PAGE:30==
  
 
 
 
22
to create new divisions and more open spaces in the apartments. EMA thinks that 
the more open structure creates a better daylight environment. 
The accessibility to the apartments have also been addressed by installing 
elevators in the stairwells and increasing the size of bathrooms etc. so they meet 
the special need for disabled people. 
In order to lower the building’s energy consumption there has been proposed an 
external insulation of the gable with reuse of the original bricks. Generally the 
facades are in a state where they need extensive rework of mortar and exchange of 
damaged bricks. The optimal solution from an energy perspective would therefore 
be to insulate all of the facades and eliminate all thermal bridges, but this idea has 
been discarded because of the large investment needed to implement the 
proposal.  
EMA is using an insulation method, where the outer layer of bricks is cut down, 
then cleaned for mortar by machines. The gable is then insulated on the outside 
and the cleaned bricks can be used as a facing wall with the same patina and 
expression as the rest of the building complex. This method eliminates thermal 
bridges in the gable and offers a sustainable solution as the bricks can be reused. 
The method is approximately 25 % more expensive than traditional external 
insulation, but with the demands for the façade expression, this solution is 
considered as the best compromise. Insulation of the gable with 100 mm mineral 
wool gives the gable-wall a U-value of 0,26 W/m2K. 
Another measure to lower the energy consumption is installation of modern low 
energy windows; their specifications can be seen in Table 4.3. There has not been 
much focus on the thermal indoor environment, which is something that will be 
addressed in this thesis.  
Table 4.3

The demonstration apartment has a room layout that allows daylight through from

room being combined to one multi-functional room. The old kitchen and bathroom 
are combined to form a new room. The bathroom is moved into the centre of the 
apartment, as an installation shaft is required for the new ventilation and 
installations. As the bathroom is a secondary room, there is no need for a window

to provide daylight. The bedroom is reduced in size to accommodate space for the

The balconies also change their layout, with the main balcony increasing in size to

indoor area, as this eliminates cold bridges and exploits an area that is largely 
unused by the residents. 
Erik Møller Architects’ main proposal consists of a changed layout of the floor plan, 
with the inclusion of the gable balcony and a more open layout with shared 
kitchen/living room space and a larger door between the northeast room and the 
living room allowing daylight through the building. Together with a more accessible 
balcony on the park side, this should enhance the daylight and increase the 
usability of the apartments.  
In addition to the changed layout, new windows and insulation of the gables 
decrease the energy demand for heating. Furthermore, the balcony construction is 
revised in order to minimise thermal bridges from the construction.

5 SIMULATION BASIS 
The demonstration apartment is used to test the ideas of EMA’s architectural ideas 
with the interior design and balcony. The test apartment is placed in the northern 
area of Højstrupparken and is a gable apartment with approximately 65 m2 floor 
area. 
The simulations of the indoor environment are performed on the test apartment 
positioned on the 2nd floor with adjacent apartments above, below and on one side. 
The simulated apartment is placed 5.6m above the ground. The properties of 
facades and windows vary between the different simulations as well as the floor 
plan layout. Specific simulation properties and floor plans for the different scenarios 
are presented under the relevant chapters. 
Figure 5.1 
Simulation setup with 
adjacent buildings and 
landscaping objects 
 
 
Figure 5.1 shows the test building and its surroundings together with the test 
apartment. The simulation building is rotated 110° clockwise with the neighbouring 
building is positioned 20 m south. The simulation models are built up, so that each 
room represents a thermal zone. 
5.1 ROOM CONDITIONS 
All simulations has the same heating profile, where the heating system is active in 
the heating season defined as the period from 1st of October to 30th of April 
(Thomsen & Aggerholm, 2000). The heating setpoint is constantly at 20°C during 
the heating season (Aggerholm & Grau, 2011). It is assumed that the heating plant 
has an unlimited heating capacity and is able to heat the room to the desired 
temperature. The integrated default HVAC heating system setting from IES-VE is 
retained. 
==PAGE:34==
  
 
 
 
26
There is not defined any cooling for the test apartment, as this is not present in the 
existing conditions, nor a wish for future use of the building.  
5.2 INTERNAL GAINS 
”SBi Anvisning 213” (Aggerholm & Grau, 2011) is the basis for the inputs to the 
simulation models. It can be estimated that residential buildings are in use 
constantly and is occupied by 2 persons. Together with the occupancy there is an 
equipment load. Table 5.1 gives an overview of the internal heat loads in the test 
apartment. 
Table 5.1 
Internal gains 
 
Load definition 
Load 
Occupants 
90 W/person 
Equipment 
3,5 W/m2 
 
5.3 AIR EXCHANGES 
The apartment is naturally ventilated; some apartments have hoods with 
mechanical exhausts in the kitchens, but that is only a minority of the apartments. 
Bathrooms do not have mechanical exhausts either. It has been reported from the 
janitor that he has observed that the residents rarely open their windows unless it is 
in warmer periods, with outdoor temperatures exceeding indoor temperatures. On 
this background, there is no ventilation during winter apart from infiltration. In 
periods with temperatures above 23°C it is assumed that the residents open their 
windows to reduce the indoor temperature (Aggerholm & Grau, 2011).  
Infiltration can be estimated with an air tightness test or alternatively by estimating 
the value using tables. As there is no air tightness test present for this case, the 
infiltration is estimated with “Håndbog for energikonsulenter” annex 4.1.2 
(Energistyrelsen, 2008). It gives an infiltration between 0,4-0,5 l/s*m2 for leaky 
buildings with a significant additional air exchange. Therefore a value of 0,4 l/s*m2 
will be used for the infiltration based on the fact that the windows are from the 
beginning of the 1980’s. It is reported that the windows have well maintained seals 
(Fritsdal, 2009), but because the building is in a state where it needs renovation, 
and with vents from kitchens, it is assumed that there are a lot of leaks contributing 
to the before mentioned infiltration value. Infiltration is assumed constant over the 
entire year. 
To simulate summer ventilation by opening the windows, a “MacroFlo” profile is set 
up in IESVE. This adds parameters to the windows of the apartment in order to 
simulate the air movement in and out of the building. External windows are opened 
depending on the profile function; gt(ta,25,4) & (to>15). The function describes that 
the windows opens gradually over a bandwidth of 4°C when the indoor air 
temperature is above 25°C. In effect this means that the windows are opened 
gradually when indoor temperatures exceed 23°C and are fully opened at 
==PAGE:35==
  
 
 
 
27
temperatures of 27°C. Windows are only opened if the outside temperature is 
higher than 15°C. Windows are estimated to be top hung windows with an 
openable area of 80 % of the window area and a maximum angle of opening of 
20°. Summer ventilation can be active in the morning between 7 and 9 in the 
morning and again in the afternoon after 15.00. This is assumed to be the period, 
where the majority of the residents are able to open their windows. As the 
composition of the residents is a mix between students, unemployed, workers etc. 
these periods are assumed to be conservative. If the residents open their windows 
between 9-15 it is possible to obtain a better cooling of the apartments, but it is not 
representative for a larger group of people, as it cannot be expected that all 
residents are home during the day. 
5.4 DAYLIGHT 
The daylight distribution has been simulated in “Velux Visualizer 2” with CIE 
standard overcast sky, that delivers 10.000 lux on an outside horizontal surface 
(Iversen et al., 2013). The daylight factor is the ratio between the illumination on an 
internal surface compared with the illumination on an unobstructed external 
surface. This gives an overview of the daylight distribution in the room as factors 
such as orientation, time or season. The daylight factor is calculated for a situation 
in Odense, with the coordinates; 55.39979, 10.34187 and angled 110° eastwards. 
Table 5.2 shows surface properties and reflectance for the building components in 
the simulated apartment.

In the summer there is a tendency towards a slightly warm sensation with peak 
periods where the temperature is experienced as warm. In the seasonal transitions 
between winter/spring and autumn/winter where the heating systems shuts down,

In the summer period when the indoor temperatures are high, is also causing PPD 
to exceed the satisfaction threshold for the perception of the indoor climate. 
6.1.4 Part conclusion 
The apartment has a large energy demand caused by old constructions with a 
large number of thermal bridges and old windows. This lead some residents to 
turning down the heat or keeping their windows closed in an attempt to save 
money for heating their apartments. By doing this they increase the risk of mould 
growth on the outer walls and especially in the areas around the thermal bridges. 
The thermal indoor environment is slightly cold in the winter and warm in the 
summer. This is a result of poor insulated climate envelope combined with 
south/south west oriented windows, which contribute with a significant solar gain. 
In order to improve the thermal indoor climate and reduce the energy demand, 
EMA has proposed insulation of the gable and changing to new and better 
insulating windows. The next chapter will focus on EMA’s solution and its 
implications on daylight, energy usage and indoor climate. To ensure that the heat 
loss for ventilation is minimised, different ventilation solutions are examined in 
chapter 6.3.

6.2 EMA RENOVATION PROPOSAL 
This section analyses the proposed solutions in order to see if they have the 
desired output in relation to energy usage and indoor climate.  
To get an overview of how the different parameters of the renovation affects the 
energy demand and indoor climate, the analysis will focus on the following 
parameter combinations: 
• 
Changed gable: Balcony is removed and included in floor area. Thermal 
properties of windows and walls remain the same as existing conditions.  
• 
Changed gable and new windows: Balcony included in floor area, new 
energy positive windows, walls with existing properties. 
• 
Changed gable + ext. insulation: Balcony included in floor area, external 
insulation on gable, existing windows. 
• 
Changed gable, ext. insulation + new windows: Balcony included in floor 
area, external insulation on gable, and new energy positive windows.

6.2.3 PMV and PPD

cold in the transition periods in spring and fall. There are fewer peaks in the graph 
in this period indicating that it is easier to keep the heat in the building. On the other 
hand there is a prolonged period where the occupants will feel slightly warm or 
warm in the summer period.

temperature in the summer period, which results in a significantly higher 
dissatisfaction in the period June to September. There are a high number of peaks,

6.2.4 Evaluation with EMA 
After the existing conditions and EMA renovation proposal had been analysed, 
there was a meeting to evaluate on the process and to establish future work 
direction.  
The data presented in this report about the existing conditions and the renovation 
proposal was shown to Morten Ørsager from EMA at November 7th. The feedback 
was that Figure 6.5 gave a good overview of the consequences of improving the 
façade on the indoor climate. It was clear for him that a reduction of energy 
demand has a significant impact on the thermal indoor environment. It was agreed 
that an increased focus on ventilation is needed in order to obtain a better indoor 
climate, as he had got an agreement with a company called “ventilationsvinduet” to 
test their windows in the demonstration apartment. Adding ventilation corresponds 
well with Real Dania and their sponsorship to the project, as they demand solutions 
that improve the indoor climate in order for them to participate with their 
contributions.  
“Ventilationsvinduet” produces windows with a cavity between two window layers 
that act as a preheater for the natural ventilation performed through the window. 
This solution can supply ventilation to the apartment, while reducing the energy 
demand for it. It was agreed that this window solution should be simulated in order 
to see its implications on the indoor environment. Another option could be to look 
at different glazing types and their effect on reducing overheating. From an 
engineering view the best solution to reduce high temperatures is to install an 
external shading system. It was also suggested to include mechanical ventilation in 
==PAGE:43==
  
 
 
 
35
order to compare the ventilation window with an already tested and working 
solution. 
The result of the meeting was that future work should focus on the following: 
• 
Increased ventilation. Can be performed by a ventilation window or 
mechanically. 
• 
Reducing solar heat in the summer. Can be performed with different 
glazing types or with shading. Possibility to test for different shading 
devices if this can be performed without radically changing the look of the 
façade. 
6.2.5 Part conclusion 
Insulation and new windows performed as expected as the energy demand is 
lowered and the indoor temperatures are increasing.  
A solution as the one EMA has come up with is therefore normal, but it underlines 
the importance of a proper process before insulating or changing components. The 
focus shall be more holistic and include parameters such as daylight and indoor 
climate and not only energy consumption as is often the case. After all we spend 
approx. 80-90 % of the time indoor and around 16 hours in our homes (Kristine 
Virén, Clasen, & Geyer, 2013). It is therefore of great importance that the indoor 
environment and air quality is of a certain quality.  
Solutions to improve the indoor climate can be to control the sun that is entering 
the apartment with different glazing or preferably with blinds. The sun adds 
significant amounts of heat during spring and autumn, and installing glazing with a 
lower g-value, will decrease this contribution together with the available daylight. 
Improved ventilation, both mechanically or naturally will also improve the indoor 
climate and reduce the energy demand. Based on conversations and meetings 
with EMA it has been agreed that the next step in order to improve the indoor 
climate will be to look at ventilation windows, which preheats the air before it enters 
the room. The different solutions are analysed and compared on the basis of their 
performance and Cost of Saved Energy (CSE). 
6.3 SOLUTIONS TO INCREASE VENTILATION 
As it can be seen from the results of EMA’s renovation proposal, there is a need to 
control the indoor environment better in order to supply the right amounts of fresh 
air prescribed by the building regulations and reducing the amounts of hours with 
overheating.  
The building regulations chapter 6.3.1.2 par. 1 states that residential building must 
have an air exchange of 0,3 l/s*m2 and shall be equipped with an extractor fan in 
==PAGE:44==
  
 
 
 
36
the kitchen (Erhvervs- og byggestyrelsen, 2009). The basic ventilation is normally 
performed by vents, drilled into the windows, to provide fresh air to the apartments. 
When the apartment is naturally ventilated the energy in the air is not reused when 
the air is vented out of the building, causing an increased heating demand in cold 
periods. Draught near windows can also be a problem in winter periods. This 
chapter will evaluate two different approaches to reducing heat loss and discomfort 
by either adding ventilation windows that preheat the air with the heat loss through 
the window glass or alternatively by installing a mechanical ventilation aggregate 
with heat recovery. 
6.3.1 Ventilation windows 
Figure 6.8 
Principle of a ventilation 
window 
(Laustsen et al., 2014) 
 
 
 
 
 
The principle of a ventilation window is shown in Figure 6.8. It functions as a 
coupled window with a ventilated cavity between the glass layers. In the top and 
bottom of the cavity, there are vents allowing outside air to pass through the 
window construction. Outside air enters the cavity from a vent in the bottom of the 
window, and is then heated by sun and heat loss from the apartment. As it is 
heated up it rises up and enters the room through a vent in the top of the window. 
A specific product from “Ventilationsvinduet” is used in this project, as they are 
supplying this type of window to the demonstration apartment that is to be built in 
the beginning of 2015. The window construction and heat loss is shown in Figure 
6.9.  
Outside 
Inside 
Preheated air 
==PAGE:45==
  
 
 
 
37
Figure 6.9 
ClimaWinTechniq type 
Wwnt 2100 with 2+1 layer 
glazing and wooden 
frame 
 
 
 
The window is constructed with a 2 layer pane on the outside coupled with a 1- 
layer pane on the inside of the ventilated cavity. Table 6.5 shows the properties for 
the window.  
Table 6.5 
Window properties 
for Wwnt 2100 (see. 
App. 6)

+5,63kWh/m2 
 
In order to simulate the window in IES-VE it is necessary to determine a 
mathematical expression that can be used to describe the heating of the outside air 
together with the air’s effect on the window’s heat loss. The report 
“Projekteringsviden om ventilationsvinduer” written in collaboration between Niras, 
DTU Byg, SBi and two window companies (Laustsen et al., 2014) has developed a 
mathematical method for implementation in a dynamic simulation tool such as IES-
VE.  
The method described in the report is suitable for testing windows with a design 
similar to the one tested at DTU. The proposed window design from 
“ventilationsvinduet” differs from the report’s design by having a 2-layer pane as the 
outer glazing and a 1-layer pane as inner glazing. This should result in marginally 
higher air temperatures and heat loss. However it is assumed that testing with a 
window design similar to the report and with properties from Table 6.5, it should be 
possible to obtain a viable estimate of how the ventilation window affects the indoor 
climate and energy consumption.  
==PAGE:46==
  
 
 
 
38
6.3.1.1 Geometry and position of windows 
If ventilation windows shall work properly, they have to be installed on a south, 
southwest or southeast façade. This means that it is possible to implement this 
window type in the kitchen/living room and in the bedroom. The windows will 
preferably have to supply pre-heated air to the entire apartment. Figure 6.10 shows 
the windows that can be replaced with ventilation windows. 
Figure 6.10 
South-west and south-
east facing windows 
suited for ventilation 
windows 
 
 
The windows in Højstrupparken are not equal in size to the European reference size 
of 1,23 x 1,48m (Energivinduer, 2014), the average glass area of the five suitable 
windows is 3,4 % smaller than the glass area of a reference window. It is assumed 
that the difference is negligible in relation to the expression for the ventilation 
window. It is unknown if window size has a linear effect on the energy savings for 
heating, but the difference will be taken into account in the simulation results

energy is found in the increased temperature of the ventilation air.

6.3.1.5 PMV and PPD 
Changes in PMV and PPD are not significant. The maximum PPD is 63,9 % at 8th 
of July, where the indoor temperature exceeds 29 °C. A yearly average of 9,23 % is 
in line with the results found in chapter 6.2. It is the same for PMV where the 
maximum value of 1,74 is found the same day, and a mean predicted vote for the 
year of 0,14. The reason that changes are not observed is because the ventilation 
rate has not been changed, but only the temperature of the ventilated air.  
6.3.2 Mechanical ventilation with heat recovery 
As the ventilation window only offers limited heat recovery of the ventilation air, it is 
tested against a mechanical system in order to evaluate, which is the best solution 
for the indoor climate, energy consumption and economy of the project. A 
decentralised ventilation system from Inventilate (Inventilate, 2014b) has been 
chosen as the comparison product. The decision is made together with EMA, as 
they have shown interest in this specific product for the demonstration apartment.  
MicroVent performs ventilation through the façade instead of ducts in the ceiling 
and through the building. A number of compact ventilation units are built into the 
façade in each room and cooperate to exchange the air. This allows for freedom in 
the placement and reduced costs for installations. The chosen aggregate has a 
heat recovery efficiency of 81 % and an energy consumption with a SEL value of 
700 J/m3 (Inventilate, 2014a). The ventilation system is simulated in IES-VE with the 
parameters described in Table 6.7. 
Table 6.7 
Input parameters for 
mechanical 
ventilation 
 
Description

8760 hours/year

0,13 l/s*m2 
Heat recovery 
81 % 
SEL-value 
700 J/m3 
 
In order to compare the ventilation window with mechanical ventilation, the 
aggregate is set to run all year. In reality it would be beneficial to have a hybrid 
system to save electricity in the summer months, as it is possible to have natural 
ventilation in periods when the outdoor temperature is equal or higher than the 
indoor temperature. The heat recovery profile is shown in Figure 6.12. 
==PAGE:50==
  
 
 
 
42
Figure 6.12 
Heat recovery profile for 
mechanical ventilation 
 
 
 
When the inside and outside temperature is below 22°C and 19°C then the heat 
recovery is active. If it is warmer then the heat exchanger is inactive and the 
apartment is ventilated with outside air. The expression that is entered into IES-VE 
is: 
if(to < 19, ramp(ta, 22, ((ta - to)*0.81), 22, to), to) 
6.3.2.1 Indoor temperatures and energy demand 
Mechanical ventilation contributes to significant lower energy consumption for 
heating as up to 81 % of the energy in the indoor air is reused when ventilating. As 
the ventilation system is driven with electricity, this has to be added the energy 
demand and therefore the gathered energy demand for heating and running of 
ventilation system is 48 kWh/m2. Electricity contributes with 4 % of the energy 
demand.  
The indoor air temperatures are not affected compared to natural ventilation, as the 
aggregate is shutting down the heat recovery when outside temperatures exceeds 
19 °C. This can be seen in Table 6.8, where the amount of hours with too high 
temperatures is the same as EMA’s proposal.

PMV and PPD are not significantly affected by mechanical ventilation. It can be

summer temperatures. In order to reduce the amount of predicted dissatisfied 
changes must be implemented in order to reduce the solar heat in warm periods.

6.3.3 Evaluation with EMA 
The result of the differences between the ventilation window and mechanical 
system was been presented the 10.12.2014. Morten Ørsager found it interesting to 
see an empirical analysis of the subject as this gives a clear indication of the effect 
of installing systems to reduce energy loss from ventilation. 
It was discussed what the possibilities are in regards to making changes to the 
outside geometry of the building. Morten Ørsager could inform that there are not 
many possibilities, but some changes to the glass area of the balcony/bay window 
could be done. A different indention of the windows in the gable can also be 
tested. Solar shading could also be a solution if they do not alter the expression of 
the façade.  
6.3.4 Part conclusion 
It can be seen that the ventilation solutions does not have an impact on the thermal 
indoor climate in the apartment. The cause is that the ventilation rate has been kept 
the same during the different simulations. By keeping the ventilation rate, it is 
possible to directly compare the different solutions. Both solutions decrease the 
energy demand for heating, by reusing energy from inside the apartment. It is done 
by two different measures, where the window depends on heat loss and solar 
radiation and the mechanical ventilation reuses heat from the indoor air. A 
comparison of the different measures can be seen on Figure 6.14. 
The ventilation window will contribute with its largest air heating around midday, as 
this is where the solar radiation is highest. In the morning and evening it is 
dependent on the heat loss through the window and the temperature rise is 
==PAGE:52==
  
 
 
 
44
therefore lower. The challenge with this solution is that the temperature rise is not 
very high, and therefore only slightly reduces the discomfort connected to cold 
draught. 
Figure 6.14 
Comparison of energy 
reduction between the 
proposals. 
 
 
Mechanical ventilation is reusing a large amount of the heat stored in the air and is 
therefore able to deliver a larger temperature rise to the benefit of both residents 
and energy consumption. The increased electricity demand contributes to a larger 
overall saving and it can therefore be considered that this energy is well spent 
6.4 SOLUTIONS TO REDUCE OVERHEATING 
As indicated in Ch. 6.2 a change of the geometry has a large impact on the indoor 
temperature, as the amount of solar insolation is highly dependent on the 
geometry. This chapter focuses on solutions that can reduce the amount of hours 
with too high temperatures. The proposed solutions are changes in building 
geometry and installation of external shading devices.

6.4.1.2 Indoor temperatures and energy demand 
The reintroduction of the gable balcony has a significant effect on solar insolation in 
the apartment. Figure 6.18 shows how the solar gain is affected during a year 
when the gable balcony is kept. It can be seen that a significant reduction is found 
in the period from May to September, where the solar gain in the apartment is 
reduced with 11,8 %. The simulation result also shows a minor increase in energy 
consumption for heating from 63,1 to 65,9 kWh/m2. A closer analysis of the 
difference shows that the difference is caused by a reduction in floor area of 2 m2, 
that causes the gain from people and equipment to be slightly reduced. Together 
with a small reduction in the solar gain in the period from October to April 
constitutes of 0,1MWh, more heat is requires the heating system to produce the 
lost solar heat.

6.4.2.3 PMV and PPD

PPD and PMV other measures are required.  
6.4.3 External solar shading 
An external solar shading system is an effective method for improving the indoor 
thermal environment. It is a commonly used tool in office buildings, but is not 
common in residential buildings. A reason for this, is that there are no demands in 
the building regulations that state that the thermal indoor climate shall comply with 
a certain standard. But as buildings are getting better insulated and sealed, the 
indoor temperatures are increasing, especially in periods where the solar insolation 
is high. While the sunlight provides heat in periods where it is needed, it also 
creates discomfort when the sun creates glare or too high indoor temperatures. 
The only way to control the indoor temperature without using energy for cooling is 
to open the windows and reduce the amount of sunlight entering the room.  
In this case, a shading system is incorporated into the façade, so the overall 
expression of the building is not affected too much. The gable and balcony is 
cladded with white concrete slats, which are imitating a latched wood cladding. 
This look has been imitated in the design of a shading device with movable louvres 
that can be angled for optimal compromise between view out and sun shading. 
Figure 6.23 is a visualisation of the design concept where the windows are 
retracted and with horizontal louvres.  
==PAGE:58==
  
 
 
 
50
Figure 6.23

louvres in same design as 
façade.

the apartment. In this example the shading system is active in the period from 9-17 
on weekdays. The modulation profile for the control of the louvre is shown in Figure

The external shading effectively reduce direct sunlight from the large gable window. 
As the external shading is built up by louvres, which reflects the sunlight, it is 
possible to maintain an acceptable daylight factor in the majority of the living room. 
The daylight factor is above 2 % in more than half of the floor area, which is the 
requirement from the regulations in order to ensure adequate daylight. The louvres 
also ensures that there is a view out when they are in function, as it is possible to 
position them in an angle that effectively blocks direct light, and ensuring that it is 
still possible to look out of the window.

reduces the amount of hours to a level that is close to the design limit.

6.4.4 Evaluation with EMA 
The solutions were presented for Morten Ørsager the 19th January 2015. He found 
the solutions interesting, but was not keen to go back to a reversed floor plan with 
a balcony in the gable. However, the idea with retracted windows could function in 
reality, as it doesn’t alter the look too much. Reusing the theme of the façade to 
create exterior shading was also seen as a good idea. The costs for implementing 
solar shading may not be realistic as parts of the costs are passed on to the 
residents by means of increased rent. The ideas will be brought to meeting with the 
resident association, where a meeting is planned in the end of January. Here the 
solutions will be discussed, and if some of them are found interesting, they could 
end up being implemented in the final project. 
 
6.4.5 Part conclusion 
Reintroduction of a gable balcony was tested in order to see how the initial design 
functioned, but the solution offered some challenges to the inside room distribution. 
Furthermore the solution would not reduce thermal bridges as the floor of the 
balcony is in direct contact with the inside of the apartment. By including the 
balcony in the inside area, it is possible to eliminate thermal bridges, increasing 
floor area and add extra insulation in the gable. The solution with retracted 
windows tries to rectify some of the overheating issues that a repositioning of the 
==PAGE:61==
  
 
 
 
53
windows has caused. The different solutions to create a shading overhang on the 
outside facade geometry seems to perform similar in order to reduce the amount of 
hours where the temperature is too high. An external movable shading device is an 
effective tool for reducing direct sunlight when it is needed, to create a pleasant 
indoor environment.

being used, and because a large number of energy investments are made. This 
creates a low risk and therefore a low interest rate.

supplier and found to be (Fjernvarme Fyn, 2015):

renovation of the gable is already prepared by EMA. Here the energy investment 
will be difference in price between removing and adding new mortar and the 
process of external insulation with new outer wall.
